Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\t\t\t\t<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=A350_XWB&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"A350" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/A350_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="A350 (disambiguation)">A350 (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n\n</p>\n<table class="infobox" airliner</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th><span class="nowrap">National origin</span>\n</th>\n<td>Multi-national<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a 2013<sup id="cite_ref-A350_1st_flight_planned_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A350_1st_flight_planned-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n\n<tr>\n<th>Introduction\n</th>\n<td>15 January 2015 with Qatar Airways<sup id="cite_ref-15_Jan_2015_EIS_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15_Jan_2015_EIS-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n\n<tr>\n<th>Status\n</th>\n<td>In production, in service\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Primary&#160;users\n</th>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Qatar_Airways" title="Qatar Airways">Qatar Airways</a><br /><a href="/wiki/Cathay_Pacific" title="Cathay Pacific">Cathay Pacific</a> <br /><a href="/wiki/Singapore_Airlines" title="Singapore Airlines">Singapore Airlines</a> <br /><a href="/wiki/China_Airlines" title="China Airlines">China Airlines</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Produced\n</th>\n<td>2010\xe2\x80\x93present<sup id="cite_ref-prodstart_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prodstart-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th><span class="nowrap">Number built</span>\n</th>\n<td>193 as of 31 August 2018<sup id="cite_ref-Airbus_O_D_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Airbus_O_D-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th><span class="nowrap">Program cost</span>\n</th>\n<td>\xe2\x82\xac11 billion<sup id="cite_ref-BBC130614_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC130614-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th><span class="nowrap">Unit cost</span>\n</th>\n<td><div>\nA350-900: US$317.4&#160;million (2018)<sup id="cite_ref-Airbus_prices_2018_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Airbus_prices_2018-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> <br />A350-1000: US$366.5&#160;million (2018)<sup id="cite_ref-Airbus_prices_2018_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Airbus_prices_2018-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup></div>\n</td></tr>\n\n\n\n</tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Airbus A350 XWB</b> is a family of <a href="/wiki/Flight_length" title="Flight length">long-range</a>, <a href="/wiki/Twinjet" title="Twinjet">twin-engine</a> <a href="/wiki/Wide-body_aircraft" title="Wide-body aircraft">wide-body</a> <a href="/wiki/Jet_airliner" title="Jet airliner">jet airliners</a> developed by European <a href="/wiki/Aerospace_manufacturer" title="Aerospace manufacturer">aerospace manufacturer</a> <a href="/wiki/Airbus" title="Airbus">Airbus</a>.  The A350 is the first Airbus aircraft with both <a href="/wiki/Fuselage" title="Fuselage">fuselage</a> and wing structures made primarily of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_fiber_reinforced_polymer" title="Carbon fiber reinforced polymer">carbon fiber reinforced polymer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-presentation_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-presentation-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Its variants seat 280 to 366 passengers in typical three-class seating layouts.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The A350 is positioned to succeed the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A340" title="Airbus A340">A340</a> and to compete with the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_787_Dreamliner" title="Boeing 787 Dreamliner">Boeing 787</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boeing_777" title="Boeing 777">777</a>.\n</p><p>The A350 was originally conceived in 2004 as a pairing of the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A330" title="Airbus A330">A330</a>\'s fuselage with new aerodynamics features and engines.  In 2006, Airbus redesigned the aircraft in response to negative feedback from several major prospective customers and renamed it the <i>A350 XWB</i> (<i>extra wide body</i>). Development costs are estimated at \xe2\x82\xac11&#160;billion (US$15&#160;billion or <a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">\xc2\xa3</a>9.5&#160;billion).<sup id="cite_ref-BBC130614_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC130614-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> As of  June&#160;2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A350_XWB&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, Airbus had received 882 orders for A350s from 45 customers worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-Airbus_O_D_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Airbus_O_D-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The prototype A350 first flew on 14 June 2013 from <a href="/wiki/Toulouse%E2%80%93Blagnac_Airport" title="Toulouse\xe2\x80\x93Blagnac Airport">Toulouse</a>, France. Type certification from the <a href="/wiki/European_Aviation_Safety_Agency" title="European Aviation Safety Agency">European Aviation Safety Agency</a> was received in September 2014 and certification from the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Aviation_Administration" title="Federal Aviation Administration">Federal Aviation Administration</a> two months later. On 15 January 2015, the A350-900 entered service with its launch operator Qatar Airways, and the A350-1000 on 24 February 2018 with the same airline.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A350_XWB&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_designs">Early designs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A350_XWB&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early designs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Airbus initially rejected Boeing\'s claim that the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_787_Dreamliner" title="Boeing 787 Dreamliner">Boeing 787 Dreamliner</a> would be a serious threat to the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A330" title="Airbus A330">Airbus A330</a>, stating that the 787 was just a reaction to the A330 and that no response was needed. When airlines urged Airbus to provide a competitor, Airbus initially proposed the "A330-200Lite", a derivative of the A330 featuring improved aerodynamics and engines similar to those on the 787.<sup id="cite_ref-Gunston_p.253_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gunston_p.253-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The company planned to announce this version at the 2004 <a href="/wiki/Farnborough_Airshow" title="Farnborough Airshow">Farnborough Airshow</a>, but did not proceed.<sup id="cite_ref-Gunston_p.253_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gunston_p.253-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Initial_Airbus_A350_concept.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/83/Initial_Airbus_A350_concept.jpg/220px-Initial_Airbus_A350_concept.jpg" width="220" height="86" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/83/Initial_Airbus_A350_concept.jpg/330px-Initial_Airbus_A350_concept.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/83/Initial_Airbus_A350_concept.jpg/440px-Initial_Airbus_A350_concept.jpg 2x" data-file-width="504" data-file-height="198" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Initial_Airbus_A350_concept.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The initial A350 concept, based on the <a href="/wiki/A330" class="mw-redirect" title="A330">A330</a></div></div></div>\n<p>On 16 September 2004, Airbus president and chief executive officer <a href="/wiki/No%C3%ABl_Forgeard" title="No\xc3\xabl Forgeard">No\xc3\xabl Forgeard</a> confirmed the consideration of a new project during a private meeting with prospective customers.<sup id="cite_ref-Gunston_p.253_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gunston_p.253-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Forgeard did not give a project name, and he did not state whether it would be an entirely new design or a modification of an existing product. Airline dissatisfaction with this proposal motivated Airbus to commit \xe2\x82\xac4&#160;billion to a new airliner design.<sup id="cite_ref-Gunston_p.253_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gunston_p.253-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The original version of the A350 superficially resembled the A330 due to its common <a href="/wiki/Fuselage" title="Fuselage">fuselage</a> cross-section and assembly. A new wing, engines, and a <a href="/wiki/Tailplane" title="Tailplane">horizontal stabiliser</a>\xe2\x80\x93to be coupled with new <a href="/wiki/Composite_material" title="Composite material">composite materials</a> and production methods applied to the fuselage\xe2\x80\x93would make the A350 an almost all-new aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-Gunston_p.253_10-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gunston_p.253-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> On 10 December 2004, the boards of EADS and <a href="/wiki/BAE_Systems" title="BAE Systems">BAE Systems</a>, then the shareholders of Airbus, gave Airbus an "authorisation to offer" (ATO) and formally named it the A350.<sup id="cite_ref-Gunston_p.253_10-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gunston_p.253-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbc_auth_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc_auth-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 13 June 2005 at the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Air_Show" title="Paris Air Show">Paris Air Show</a>, Middle Eastern carrier <a href="/wiki/Qatar_Airways" title="Qatar Airways">Qatar Airways</a> announced that they had placed an order for 60 A350s. In September 2006 the airline signed a <a href="/wiki/Memorandum_of_understanding" title="Memorandum of understanding">memorandum of understanding</a> with General Electric to launch the GEnx-1A-72 engine for the aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-Qatar_signs_MoU_to_launch_GEnx_on_A350_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Qatar_signs_MoU_to_launch_GEnx_on_A350-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Airbus_unleashes_A350_for_long-range_twin_dogfight_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Airbus_unleashes_A350_for_long-range_twin_dogfight-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-A350_lifts_off_with_$15bn_Qatar_order_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A350_lifts_off_with_$15bn_Qatar_order-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Emirates_(airline)" title="Emirates (airline)">Emirates</a> sought a more improved design and decided against ordering the initial version of the A350.<sup id="cite_ref-cancelled_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cancelled-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 6 October 2005, the programme\'s industrial launch was announced with an estimated development cost of around \xe2\x82\xac3.5&#160;billion.<sup id="cite_ref-Gunston_p.253_10-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gunston_p.253-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>  The A350 was initially planned to be a 250- to 300-seat twin-engine <a href="/wiki/Wide-body_aircraft" title="Wide-body aircraft">wide-body aircraft</a> derived from the existing A330\'s design. Under this plan, the A350 would have modified wings and new engines while sharing the A330\'s fuselage cross-section. As a result of a controversial design, the fuselage was to consist primarily of <a href="/wiki/Aluminium-lithium_alloy" class="mw-redirect" title="Aluminium-lithium alloy">aluminium-lithium</a> rather than the <a href="/wiki/Carbon-fiber-reinforced_polymer" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon-fiber-reinforced polymer">carbon-fiber-reinforced polymer</a> (CFRP) fuselage on the Boeing 787. The A350 would see entry in two versions: the A350-800 with a 8,800&#160;nmi (16,300&#160;km) range with a typical passenger capacity of 253 in a three-class configuration, and the A350-900 with 7,500&#160;nmi (13,900&#160;km) range and a 300-seat 3-class configuration. The A350 was designed to be a direct competitor to the Boeing 787-9 and <a href="/wiki/Boeing_777#777-200ER" title="Boeing 777">777-200ER</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gunston_p.253_10-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gunston_p.253-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The A350 was publicly criticised by two of Airbus\' largest customers, <a href="/wiki/International_Lease_Finance_Corporation" title="International Lease Finance Corporation">International Lease Finance Corporation</a> (ILFC) and <a href="/wiki/GE_Capital_Aviation_Services" title="GE Capital Aviation Services">GE Capital Aviation Services</a> (GECAS). On 28 March 2006, ILFC President <a href="/wiki/Steven_F._Udvar-H%C3%A1zy" title="Steven F. Udvar-H\xc3\xa1zy">Steven F. Udvar-H\xc3\xa1zy</a> urged Airbus to pursue a clean-sheet design or risk losing market share to Boeing and branded Airbus\'s strategy as "a Band-aid reaction to the 787", a sentiment echoed by GECAS president Henry Hubschman.<sup id="cite_ref-spi_20060329_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spi_20060329-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-leeham_20060404_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leeham_20060404-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> In April 2006, while reviewing bids for the Boeing 787 and A350, CEO of <a href="/wiki/Singapore_Airlines" title="Singapore Airlines">Singapore Airlines</a> (SIA) <a href="/wiki/Chew_Choon_Seng" title="Chew Choon Seng">Chew Choon Seng</a>, commented that "having gone through the trouble of designing a new wing, tail, cockpit... [Airbus] should have gone the whole hog and designed a new fuselage."<sup id="cite_ref-wsj_sq_20060407_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj_sq_20060407-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Airbus responded that they were considering A350 improvements to satisfy customer demands. Airbus\'s then-CEO <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Humbert" title="Gustav Humbert">Gustav Humbert</a> stated, "Our strategy isn\'t driven by the needs of the next one or two campaigns, but rather by a long-term view of the market and our ability to deliver on our promises."<sup id="cite_ref-ap_20060410_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ap_20060410-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbg_20060411_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbg_20060411-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> As major airlines such as Qantas and Singapore Airlines selected the 787 over the A350, Humbert tasked an engineering team to produce new alternative designs.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-1d_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1d-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> One such proposal, known internally as "1d", formed the basis of the A350 redesign.<sup id="cite_ref-1d_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1d-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Redesign_and_launch">Redesign and launch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A350_XWB&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Redesign and launch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A350XWB_model_ILA_2008.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/A350XWB_model_ILA_2008.png/220px-A350XWB_model_ILA_2008.png" width="220" height="100" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/A350XWB_model_ILA_2008.png/330px-A350XWB_model_ILA_2008.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/A350XWB_model_ILA_2008.png/440px-A350XWB_model_ILA_2008.png 2x" data-file-width="1358" data-file-height="620" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A350XWB_model_ILA_2008.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A model of the new design at <a href="/wiki/ILA_Berlin_Air_Show" title="ILA Berlin Air Show">ILA Berlin Air Show</a> 2008</div></div></div>\n<p>On 14 July 2006, during the Farnborough Airshow, the redesigned aircraft was designated "A350 XWB" (Xtra-Wide-Body).<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Within four days, Singapore Airlines agreed to order 20 A350XWBs with <a href="/wiki/Option_(aircraft_purchasing)" title="Option (aircraft purchasing)">options</a> for another 20 A350XWBs.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The proposed A350 was a new design, including a wider fuselage cross-section, allowing seating arrangements ranging from an eight-abreast low-density premium economy layout to a ten-abreast high-density seating configuration for a maximum <a href="/wiki/Seating_capacity" title="Seating capacity">seating capacity</a> of 440\xe2\x80\x93475 depending on variant.<sup id="cite_ref-a350_cabin_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-a350_cabin-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The A330 and previous iterations of the A350 would only be able to accommodate a maximum of eight seats per row. The 787 is typically configured for nine seats per row.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The 777 accommodates nine or ten seats per row, with more than half of recent 777s being ten-abreast as the 777X will be.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The A350 cabin is 12.7&#160;cm (5.0&#160;in) wider at the eye level of a seated passenger than the 787\'s cabin,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> and 28&#160;cm (11&#160;in) narrower than the Boeing 777\'s cabin. (See <i><a href="/wiki/Wide-body_aircraft" title="Wide-body aircraft">Wide-body aircraft</a></i> for a comparison of cabin widths and seating.) All A350 passenger models will have a range of at least 8,000&#160;nmi (14,816&#160;km). The redesigned composite fuselage provides higher cabin pressure and humidity, and lower maintenance costs.\n</p><p>On 1 December 2006, the Airbus board of directors approved the industrial launch of the A350-800, -900, and -1000 variants.<sup id="cite_ref-eads_pr_20061201_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eads_pr_20061201-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The delayed launch decision was a result of delays of the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A380" title="Airbus A380">Airbus A380</a><sup id="cite_ref-fi_20061002_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fi_20061002-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> and discussions on how to fund development. EADS CEO <a href="/wiki/Tom_Enders" title="Tom Enders">Thomas Enders</a> stated that the A350 programme was not a certainty, citing EADS/Airbus\'s stretched resources.<sup id="cite_ref-bbg_20061005_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbg_20061005-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-forbes_20061005_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-forbes_20061005-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> However, it was decided programme costs are to be borne mainly from cash-flow. First delivery for the A350-900 was scheduled for mid-2013, with the -800 and -1000 following on 12 and 24 months later, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-eads_pr_20061201_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eads_pr_20061201-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> New technical details of the A350 XWB were revealed at a press conference in December 2006. John Leahy indicated existing A350 contracts were being re-negotiated due to price increases compared to the original A350s contracted. On 4 January 2007, <a href="/wiki/Pegasus_Aviation_Finance_Company" title="Pegasus Aviation Finance Company">Pegasus Aviation Finance Company</a> placed the first firm order for the A350 XWB with an order for two aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-eads_pr_20070104_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eads_pr_20070104-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The design change imposed a two-year delay into the original timetable and increased development costs from US$5.3 billion (\xe2\x82\xac5.5 billion) to approximately US$10 billion (\xe2\x82\xac9.7 billion).<sup id="cite_ref-bbg_dev_20060717_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbg_dev_20060717-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbg_dev_20061204_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbg_dev_20061204-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The total development cost for the A350 was estimated at US$15 billion by <i>Reuters</i> (\xe2\x82\xac12 billion or \xc2\xa310 billion).<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated6_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated6-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The original mid-2013 delivery date of the A350 changed, as a longer than anticipated development forced Airbus to delay the final assembly and first flight of the aircraft to the third quarter of 2012 and second quarter of 2013 respectively. As a result, the flight test schedule was compressed from the original 15 months to 12 months. A350 programme chief Didier Evrard stressed that delays only affected the A350-900 while the -800 and -1000 schedules remained unchanged.<sup id="cite_ref-aw_a350delay_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aw_a350delay-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Design_phase">Design phase</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A350_XWB&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Design phase">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A350xwb_nose_2009B.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/A350xwb_nose_2009B.png/220px-A350xwb_nose_2009B.png" width="220" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/A350xwb_nose_2009B.png/330px-A350xwb_nose_2009B.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/A350xwb_nose_2009B.png/440px-A350xwb_nose_2009B.png 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="384" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A350xwb_nose_2009B.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A350 XWB new nose and general arrangement inside forward fuselage</div></div></div>\n<p>Airbus suggested Boeing\'s use of <a href="/wiki/Composite_material" title="Composite material">composite materials</a> for the 787 fuselage was premature, and that the new A350 XWB was to feature large <a href="/wiki/Carbon-fiber-reinforced_polymer" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon-fiber-reinforced polymer">carbon fibre</a> panels for the main fuselage skin. After facing criticism for maintenance costs,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Airbus confirmed in early September 2007 the adoption of composite fuselage frames for the aircraft structure.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The composite frames would feature aluminium strips to ensure the electrical continuity of the fuselage (for dissipating lightning strikes).<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Airbus used a full <a href="/wiki/Mock_up" class="mw-redirect" title="Mock up">mock up</a> fuselage to develop the wiring, a different approach from the A380, on which the wiring was all done on computers.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2006, Airbus confirmed development of a full <a href="/wiki/Bleed_air" title="Bleed air">bleed air</a> system on the A350, as opposed to the 787\'s bleedless configuration.<sup id="cite_ref-flugrevue_200609_xwb_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flugrevue_200609_xwb-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_plc" class="mw-redirect" title="Rolls-Royce plc">Rolls-Royce</a> agreed with Airbus to supply a new variant of the <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Trent" title="Rolls-Royce Trent">Trent</a> <a href="/wiki/Turbofan" title="Turbofan">turbofan</a> engine for the A350 XWB, named <i><a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Trent_XWB" title="Rolls-Royce Trent XWB">Trent XWB</a></i>. In 2010, after low-speed wind tunnel tests, Airbus finalized the static thrust at sea level for all three proposed variants to the 330\xe2\x80\x93420&#160;kN (74,000\xe2\x80\x9394,000&#160;lbf) range.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/GE_Aviation" title="GE Aviation">General Electric</a> (GE) stated it would not offer the <a href="/wiki/Engine_Alliance_GP7000" title="Engine Alliance GP7000">GP7000</a> engine on the aircraft, and that previous contracts for the <a href="/wiki/General_Electric_GEnx" title="General Electric GEnx">GEnx</a> on the original A350 did not apply to the XWB.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Engine_Alliance" title="Engine Alliance">Engine Alliance</a> partner <a href="/wiki/Pratt_%26_Whitney" title="Pratt &amp; Whitney">Pratt &amp; Whitney</a> seemed to be at odds with GE on this, having publicly stating that it was looking at an advanced derivative of the GP7000.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> In April 2007, Airbus former chief executive Louis Gallois held direct talks with GE management over developing a GEnx variant for the A350 XWB.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> In June 2007, Airbus\'s chief operating officer John Leahy indicated that the A350 XWB would not feature the GEnx engine, saying that Airbus wanted GE to offer a more efficient version for the airliner.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Since then, the largest GE engines operators, which include Emirates, <a href="/wiki/US_Airways" title="US Airways">US Airways</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_Airlines" title="Hawaiian Airlines">Hawaiian Airlines</a> and ILFC have selected the Trent XWB for their A350 orders. In May 2009, GE said that if it were to reach a deal with Airbus to offer the current 787-optimised GEnx for the A350, it would only power the -800 and -900 variants. GE believed it can offer a product that outperforms the Trent 1000 and Trent XWB, but was reluctant to support an aircraft competing directly with its GE90-115B-powered 777 variants.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In January 2008, French-based <a href="/wiki/Thales_Group" title="Thales Group">Thales Group</a> won a US$2.9&#160;billion (\xe2\x82\xac2&#160;billion) 20-year contract to supply avionics and navigation equipment for the A350 XWB, beating <a href="/wiki/Honeywell" title="Honeywell">Honeywell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rockwell_Collins" title="Rockwell Collins">Rockwell Collins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-thales_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thales-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> US-based <a href="/wiki/Rockwell_Collins" title="Rockwell Collins">Rockwell Collins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moog_Inc" class="mw-redirect" title="Moog Inc">Moog Inc</a> were chosen to supply the horizontal stabiliser actuator and primary flight control actuation, respectively. The flight management system incorporated several new safety features.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Regarding cabin ergonomics and entertainment, in 2006 Airbus signed a firm contract with <a href="/wiki/BMW" title="BMW">BMW</a> for development of an interior concept for the original A350.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> On 4 February 2010, Airbus signed a contract with <a href="/wiki/Panasonic_Avionics_Corporation" title="Panasonic Avionics Corporation">Panasonic Avionics Corporation</a> to deliver in-flight entertainment and communication (IFEC) systems for the Airbus A350 XWB.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A350_XWB&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Airbus_A350-941_on_the_assembly_line_in_Toulouse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Airbus_A350-941_on_the_assembly_line_in_Toulouse.jpg/220px-Airbus_A350-941_on_the_assembly_line_in_Toulouse.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Airbus_A350-941_on_the_assembly_line_in_Toulouse.jpg/330px-Airbus_A350-941_on_the_assembly_line_in_Toulouse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Airbus_A350-941_on_the_assembly_line_in_Toulouse.jpg/440px-Airbus_A350-941_on_the_assembly_line_in_Toulouse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1067" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Airbus_A350-941_on_the_assembly_line_in_Toulouse.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A partially-complete A350-941, destined for <a href="/wiki/Finnair" title="Finnair">Finnair</a>, on the Toulouse <a href="/wiki/Assembly_line" title="Assembly line">assembly line</a>, December 2014</div></div></div>\n<p>In 2008, Airbus planned to introduce new techniques and procedures to cut assembly time in half.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The A350 XWB production programme sees extensive international collaboration and investments in new facilities: Airbus constructed 10 new factories in Western Europe and the US, with extensions carried out on 3 further sites.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Among the new buildings was a \xc2\xa3570&#160;million (US$760&#160;million or \xe2\x82\xac745&#160;million) composite facility in <a href="/wiki/Airbus_Broughton" class="mw-redirect" title="Airbus Broughton">Broughton</a>, Wales, which would be responsible for the wings.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> In June 2009, the <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_for_Wales" title="National Assembly for Wales">National Assembly for Wales</a> announced provision of a \xc2\xa328&#160;million grant to provide a training centre, production jobs and money toward the new production centre.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Airbus manufactured the first structural component in December 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Production of the first fuselage barrel began in late 2010 at its production plant in <a href="/wiki/Illescas,_Toledo" title="Illescas, Toledo">Illescas</a>, Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Construction of the first A350-900 centre wingbox was set to start in August 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The new composite rudder plant in China opened in early 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The forward fuselage of the first A350 was delivered to the final assembly plant in Toulouse on 29 December 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-First_A350_ride_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-First_A350_ride-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Final assembly of the first A350 static test model was started on 5 April 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Final assembly of the first prototype A350 was completed in December 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The production rate was expected to rise from three aircraft per month in early 2015 to five at the end of 2015, and would ramp to ten aircraft per month by 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-FI141223_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FI141223-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> In 2015, 17 planes would be delivered and the initial dispatch reliability was 98%.<sup id="cite_ref-LH150423_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LH150423-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Airbus plans to increase its production rate from 10 monthly in 2018 to 13 monthly from 2019, while the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_787" class="mw-redirect" title="Boeing 787">Boeing 787</a> production will increase from 12 to 14 per month in 2019, and six <a href="/wiki/A330" class="mw-redirect" title="A330">A330</a> are produced monthly.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\nAround 90 deliveries are expected in 2018, with about 15% -1000s (\xe2\x89\x8814).<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Testing_and_certification">Testing and certification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A350_XWB&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Testing and certification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A350_First_Flight_-_Low_pass_02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/A350_First_Flight_-_Low_pass_02.jpg/220px-A350_First_Flight_-_Low_pass_02.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/A350_First_Flight_-_Low_pass_02.jpg/330px-A350_First_Flight_-_Low_pass_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/A350_First_Flight_-_Low_pass_02.jpg/440px-A350_First_Flight_-_Low_pass_02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1777" data-file-height="1167" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A350_First_Flight_-_Low_pass_02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Prototype F-WXWB during first flight</div></div></div>\n<p>The first Trent engine test was made on 14 June 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The Trent XWB\'s flight test programme began use on the A380 development aircraft in early 2011, ahead of engine certification in late 2011. On 2 June 2013, the Trent XWB engines were powered up on the A350 for the first time. Airbus confirmed that the flight test programme would last 12 months and use five test aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-busin-week_power-up_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-busin-week_power-up-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The A350\'s maiden flight took place on 14 June 2013 from the <a href="/wiki/Toulouse%E2%80%93Blagnac_Airport" title="Toulouse\xe2\x80\x93Blagnac Airport">Toulouse\xe2\x80\x93Blagnac Airport</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-A350_first_flight_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A350_first_flight-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Airbus\'s chief test pilot said, "it just seemed really happy in the air...all the things we were testing had no major issues at all."<sup id="cite_ref-A350_first_flight_pilot_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A350_first_flight_pilot-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> It flew for 4 hours, reaching Mach .8 at 25,000 feet after retracting the landing gear and starting a 2,500 h flight test campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A350 XWB, F-WWCF, msn. 2, underwent two-and-a-half weeks of climatic tests in the unique <a href="/wiki/McKinley_Climatic_Laboratory" title="McKinley Climatic Laboratory">McKinley Climatic Laboratory</a> at <a href="/wiki/Eglin_Air_Force_Base" title="Eglin Air Force Base">Eglin Air Force Base</a>, Florida, in May 2014, and was subjected to multiple climatic and humidity settings from a high of 45&#160;\xc2\xb0C to as low as -40&#160;\xc2\xb0C.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The A350 received type certification from the <a href="/wiki/European_Aviation_Safety_Agency" title="European Aviation Safety Agency">European Aviation Safety Agency</a> (EASA) on 30 September 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> On 15 October 2014, EASA approved the A350-900 for <a href="/wiki/ETOPS" title="ETOPS">ETOPS</a> 370, allowing it to fly more than six hours on one engine and making it the first airliner to be approved for "ETOPS Beyond 180 minutes" before entry into service.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Later that month Airbus received regulatory approval for a Common Type Rating for pilot training between the A350 XWB and A330.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> On 12 November 2014, the A350 received certification from the US <a href="/wiki/Federal_Aviation_Administration" title="Federal Aviation Administration">Federal Aviation Administration</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Introduction_and_early_operations">Introduction and early operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A350_XWB&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Introduction and early operations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Qatar_Airways_Airbus_A350-941_A7-ALA_15.Jan.2015_First_commercial_service_Doha-Frankfurt_(16260395846).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Qatar_Airways_Airbus_A350-941_A7-ALA_15.Jan.2015_First_commercial_service_Doha-Frankfurt_%2816260395846%29.jpg/220px-Qatar_Airways_Airbus_A350-941_A7-ALA_15.Jan.2015_First_commercial_service_Doha-Frankfurt_%2816260395846%29.jpg" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Qatar_Airways_Airbus_A350-941_A7-ALA_15.Jan.2015_First_commercial_service_Doha-Frankfurt_%2816260395846%29.jpg/330px-Qatar_Airways_Airbus_A350-941_A7-ALA_15.Jan.2015_First_commercial_service_Doha-Frankfurt_%2816260395846%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Qatar_Airways_Airbus_A350-941_A7-ALA_15.Jan.2015_First_commercial_service_Doha-Frankfurt_%2816260395846%29.jpg/440px-Qatar_Airways_Airbus_A350-941_A7-ALA_15.Jan.2015_First_commercial_service_Doha-Frankfurt_%2816260395846%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2220" data-file-height="1249" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Qatar_Airways_Airbus_A350-941_A7-ALA_15.Jan.2015_First_commercial_service_Doha-Frankfurt_(16260395846).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Qatar Airways A350-941 (A7-ALA) after the first commercial flight to <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt_Airport" title="Frankfurt Airport">Frankfurt Airport</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In June 2011, the A350-900 was scheduled to enter service in the first half of 2014, with the -800 to enter service in mid-2016, and the -1000 in 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-PARIS_A350-1000_delayed_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PARIS_A350-1000_delayed-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> In July 2012, Airbus delayed the -900\'s introduction by three months to the second half of 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_-1000_2012_delay_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_-1000_2012_delay-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> The first delivery to launch customer Qatar Airways took place on 22 December 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The first commercial flight was made on 15 January 2015 between <a href="/wiki/Hamad_International_Airport" title="Hamad International Airport">Doha</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt_Airport" title="Frankfurt Airport">Frankfurt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-15_Jan_2015_EIS_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15_Jan_2015_EIS-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>One year after introduction, the A350 fleet had accumulated 3,000 cycles and around 16,000 flight hours. Average daily usage by first customers was 11.4 hours with flights averaging 5.2 hours, which are under the aircraft\'s capabilities and reflect both short flights within the schedules of Qatar Airways and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_Airlines" title="Vietnam Airlines">Vietnam Airlines</a>, as well as flight-crew proficiency training that is typical of early use and is accomplished on short-haul flights. <a href="/wiki/Finnair" title="Finnair">Finnair</a> was operating the A350 at very high rates: 15 flight hours per day for <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>, 18 hours for <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a>, and more than 20 hours for <a href="/wiki/Bangkok" title="Bangkok">Bangkok</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AW160119_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AW160119-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> This may have accelerated its retirement of the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A340" title="Airbus A340">Airbus A340</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AW160119_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AW160119-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In service problems had included three areas. The onboard <a href="/wiki/Maintenance,_repair,_and_operations" class="mw-redirect" title="Maintenance, repair, and operations">Maintenance, Repair, Overhaul</a> network needed software improvements. Airbus issued <a href="/wiki/Technical_Service_Bulletin" title="Technical Service Bulletin">service bulletins</a> regarding onboard equipment and removed galley inserts (coffee makers, toaster ovens) because of leaks. Airbus had to address spurious overheating warnings in the bleed air system by retrofitting an original connector with a gold-plated connector. Airbus targeted a 98.5 percent dependability by the end of 2016 and to match the mature A330 reliability by early 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-AW160119_88-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AW160119-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the end of May 2016, the in-service fleet had flown 55,200 hours over 9,400 cycles at a 97.8 percent operational reliability on three months. The longest operated sector was Qatar Airways\' <a href="/wiki/Adelaide" title="Adelaide">Adelaide</a>\xe2\x80\x93<a href="/wiki/Doha" title="Doha">Doha</a> at 13.8 hours for 6,120&#160;nmi (11,334&#160;km). 45 percent of flights were under 3,000&#160;nmi (5,556&#160;km), 16 percent over 5,000&#160;nmi (9,260&#160;km), and 39 percent in between. The average flight was 6.8 hours, with the longest average being 9.6 hours by <a href="/wiki/TAM_Airlines" class="mw-redirect" title="TAM Airlines">TAM Airlines</a> and the shortest being 2.1 hours by Cathay Pacific\'s. <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_seat_configuration" class="mw-redirect" title="Aircraft seat configuration">Aircraft seat configuration</a> ranged from 253 seats for Singapore Airlines to 348 seats for TAM Airlines, with a 30 to 46 seat business class and a 211 to 318 seat economy class, often including a premium economy.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first A350-1000 was assembled in 2016, for a first flight on 24 November and entry into service planned for mid-2017.<sup id="cite_ref-FG-1000FirstFlight_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FG-1000FirstFlight-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In January 2017, two years after introduction, 62 aircraft were in service with 10 airlines. They had accumulated 25,000 flights over 154,000 hours with an average daily utilisation of 12.5 hours, and transported six million passengers with a 98.7 percent operational reliability.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zodiac_Aerospace" title="Zodiac Aerospace">Zodiac Aerospace</a> had business class seats production difficulties in its Gainesville, Texas and Santa Maria, California factories. After a year, Cathay Pacific experienced cosmetic quality issues and upgraded or replaced the seats for the earliest cabins.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2017, average <a href="/wiki/Test_flight" class="mw-redirect" title="Test flight">test flights</a> before delivery decreased to 4.1 from 12 in 2014, with an average delay down to 25 days from 68.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Its reliability was 97.2 percent in 2015, 98.3 percent in 2016, and 98.8 percent in June 2017, just behind its 99 percent target for 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In June 2017 after 30 months in commercial operation, 80 A350 were in service with 12 operators, the largest being Qatar Airways with 17 and 13 each at Cathay Pacific and Singapore Airlines (SIA).<sup id="cite_ref-Flight14June2017_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight14June2017-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> The fleet average block time was 7.2 hours with 53 percent below 3,000&#160;nmi (5,556&#160;km), 16 percent over 5,000&#160;nmi (9,260&#160;km), and 31 percent in between. <a href="/wiki/LATAM_Airlines" class="mw-redirect" title="LATAM Airlines">LATAM Airlines</a> had the longest average sector at 10.7 hours, and <a href="/wiki/Asiana_Airlines" title="Asiana Airlines">Asiana</a> had the shortest at 3.8 hours.<sup id="cite_ref-Flight14June2017_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight14June2017-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Singapore Airlines operated the longest leg, Singapore to San Francisco 7,340&#160;nmi (13,594&#160;km), and the shortest leg, Singapore to Kuala Lumpur 160&#160;nmi (296&#160;km).<sup id="cite_ref-Flight14June2017_95-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight14June2017-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> In 2016, 49 were delivered and the monthly rate should grow to 10 by the end of 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-Flight14June2017_95-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight14June2017-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Seating varied from 253 for Singapore Airlines to 389 for <a href="/wiki/Air_Cara%C3%AFbes" title="Air Cara\xc3\xafbes">Air Cara\xc3\xafbes</a>, with most between 280 and 320.<sup id="cite_ref-Flight14June2017_95-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight14June2017-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In October 2017, Airbus was testing extended <a href="/wiki/Wingtip_device" title="Wingtip device">sharklets</a>, which could offer 100\xe2\x80\x93140&#160;nmi (185\xe2\x80\x93259&#160;km) extra range and reduce fuel burn by 1.4\xe2\x80\x931.6 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\nThe wing twist is being changed for the wider, optimised spanload <a href="/wiki/Pressure_distribution" class="mw-redirect" title="Pressure distribution">pressure distribution</a>, and they will be used for the Singapore Airlines A350-900ULR in 2018 before spreading to other variants.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Iberia" class="mw-redirect" title="Iberia">Iberia</a> will be the first to get the upgraded -900 in June 2018, with a 280&#160;t (617,294&#160;lb) <a href="/wiki/MTOW" class="mw-redirect" title="MTOW">MTOW</a> version for a 8,200&#160;nmi (15,186&#160;km) range with 325 passengers in three classes.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As of February 2018, 142 -900 had been delivered, with a dispatch reliability of 99.3 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-AvWeek5feb2018_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AvWeek5feb2018-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Design">Design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A350_XWB&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A350XWB-Demo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/A350XWB-Demo.jpg/220px-A350XWB-Demo.jpg" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/A350XWB-Demo.jpg/330px-A350XWB-Demo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/A350XWB-Demo.jpg/440px-A350XWB-Demo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3985" data-file-height="3165" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A350XWB-Demo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Airbus A350 XWB product demonstrator in 2015</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Airbus_A350_Aerolia_Troncon11_LeBourget2013_2092.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Airbus_A350_Aerolia_Troncon11_LeBourget2013_2092.JPG/220px-Airbus_A350_Aerolia_Troncon11_LeBourget2013_2092.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Airbus_A350_Aerolia_Troncon11_LeBourget2013_2092.JPG/330px-Airbus_A350_Aerolia_Troncon11_LeBourget2013_2092.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Airbus_A350_Aerolia_Troncon11_LeBourget2013_2092.JPG/440px-Airbus_A350_Aerolia_Troncon11_LeBourget2013_2092.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3872" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Airbus_A350_Aerolia_Troncon11_LeBourget2013_2092.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Aerolia" class="mw-redirect" title="Aerolia">Aerolia</a> cockpit section</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A350_XWB&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In September 2007, Airbus rolled out new design advances to a gathering of 100 representatives from existing and potential XWB customers. The A350 XWB is based on the technologies developed for the Airbus A380 and includes a similar cockpit and <a href="/wiki/Fly-by-wire" title="Fly-by-wire">fly-by-wire</a> systems layout.\n</p><p>Airbus expects 10 percent lower airframe maintenance compared with the original A350 design and 14 percent lower empty seat weight than the Boeing 777.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Design freeze for the A350-900 was achieved in December 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Materials">Materials</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A350_XWB&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Materials">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The A350 XWB <a href="/wiki/Airframe" title="Airframe">airframe</a> will be made out of 53 percent composites: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_fibre_reinforced_plastic" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon fibre reinforced plastic">carbon fibre reinforced plastic</a> for the outer and centre wing box (covers, stringers, spars), fuselage (skin, frame, keel beam, and rear fuselage) and the empennage (horizontal and vertical tailplanes); 19 percent aluminium and <a href="/wiki/Aluminum-lithium_alloy" class="mw-redirect" title="Aluminum-lithium alloy">aluminum-lithium alloy</a> for ribs, floor beams, and gear bays; 14 percent <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a> for landing gears, pylons, and attachments; 6 percent steel; and 8 percent miscellaneous.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The A350\'s competitor, the Boeing 787, is 50 percent composites, 20 percent aluminium, 15 percent titanium, 10 percent steel, and 5 percent other.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuselage">Fuselage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A350_XWB&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Fuselage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A350_cabin_Finnair.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/A350_cabin_Finnair.jpg/220px-A350_cabin_Finnair.jpg" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/A350_cabin_Finnair.jpg/330px-A350_cabin_Finnair.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/A350_cabin_Finnair.jpg/440px-A350_cabin_Finnair.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1154" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A350_cabin_Finnair.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nine-abreast economy-class cabin</div></div></div>\n<p>The A350 XWB fuselage has a constant width from door 1 to door 4, unlike previous Airbus aircraft, to provide maximum usable volume.<sup id="cite_ref-Gunston_p.257_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gunston_p.257-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> The double-lobe (<a href="/wiki/Oval" title="Oval">ovoid</a>) fuselage <a href="/wiki/Cross_section_(geometry)" title="Cross section (geometry)">cross-section</a> has a maximum outer diameter of 5.97&#160;m (19.6&#160;ft), compared to 5.64&#160;m (18.5&#160;ft) for the A330/A340.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> The cabin\'s internal width is 5.61&#160;m (18.4&#160;ft) at armrest level compared to 5.49&#160;m (18.0&#160;ft) in the Boeing 787<sup id="cite_ref-A350XWB_cabin_mock_up_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A350XWB_cabin_mock_up-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> and 5.87&#160;m (19.3&#160;ft) in the Boeing 777. It allows for an eight-abreast 2\xe2\x80\x934\xe2\x80\x932 arrangement in a <a href="/wiki/Premium_economy" title="Premium economy">premium economy</a> layout, with the seats being 49.5&#160;cm (19.5&#160;in) wide between 5&#160;cm (2.0&#160;in) wide arm rests. Airbus says that the seat will be 1.3&#160;cm (0.5&#160;in) wider than a 787 seat in the equivalent configuration. In the nine-abreast, 3\xe2\x80\x933\xe2\x80\x933 standard economy layout, the A350 seat will be 45&#160;cm (18&#160;in) wide, 1.27&#160;cm (0.5&#160;in) wider than a seat in the equivalent layout in the 787,<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> and 3.9&#160;cm (1.5&#160;in) wider than a seat in the equivalent A330 layout.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> The current 777 and future derivatives have 1.27&#160;cm (0.5&#160;in) greater seat width than the A350 in a nine-abreast configuration.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> The 10-abreast seating on the A350 is similar to a 9-abreast configuration on the A330, with a seat width of 41.65&#160;cm (16.4&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-a350_cabin_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-a350_cabin-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> Overall, Airbus promises passengers more headroom, larger overhead storage space, and wider panoramic windows than current Airbus models.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DSC_8003-F-WZGG_-_MSN_003_(10512991673).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/DSC_8003-F-WZGG_-_MSN_003_%2810512991673%29.jpg/220px-DSC_8003-F-WZGG_-_MSN_003_%2810512991673%29.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/DSC_8003-F-WZGG_-_MSN_003_%2810512991673%29.jpg/330px-DSC_8003-F-WZGG_-_MSN_003_%2810512991673%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/DSC_8003-F-WZGG_-_MSN_003_%2810512991673%29.jpg/440px-DSC_8003-F-WZGG_-_MSN_003_%2810512991673%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4161" data-file-height="2774" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DSC_8003-F-WZGG_-_MSN_003_(10512991673).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Airbus A350\'s nose</div></div></div>\n<p>The A350 nose section has a configuration derived from the A380 with a forward-mounted nosegear bay and a six-panel flightdeck windscreen.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> This differs substantially from the four-window arrangement in the original design.<sup id="cite_ref-Gunston_p.258_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gunston_p.258-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> The new nose improves aerodynamics and enables overhead crew rest areas to be installed further forward and eliminate any encroachment in the passenger cabin. The new windscreen has been revised to improve vision by reducing the width of the centre post. The upper shell radius of the nose section has been increased. The nose is likely to be constructed from aluminium but Airbus is currently running trade-off studies considering a one-piece carbon fibre structure. According to Gordon McConnell, A350 Chief Engineer, a carbon fibre structure would need titanium reinforcements for birdstrike protection, thus the aluminium structure is the best cost-wise.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Undercarriage">Undercarriage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A350_XWB&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Undercarriage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Airbus_A350_Nose_Landing_Gear_Mock-up.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Airbus_A350_Nose_Landing_Gear_Mock-up.jpg/170px-Airbus_A350_Nose_Landing_Gear_Mock-up.jpg" width="170" height="256" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Airbus_A350_Nose_Landing_Gear_Mock-up.jpg/255px-Airbus_A350_Nose_Landing_Gear_Mock-up.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Airbus_A350_Nose_Landing_Gear_Mock-up.jpg/340px-Airbus_A350_Nose_Landing_Gear_Mock-up.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1860" data-file-height="2800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Airbus_A350_Nose_Landing_Gear_Mock-up.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mock-up of the A350 nose landing gear at ILA 2012</div></div></div>\n<p>Airbus adopted a new philosophy for the attachment of the A350\'s main <a href="/wiki/Landing_gear" title="Landing gear">undercarriage</a> as part of the switch to a composite wing structure. Each main undercarriage leg is attached to the rear wing spar forward and to a gear beam aft, which itself is attached to the wing and the fuselage. To help reduce the loads further into the wing, a double side-stay configuration has been adopted. This solution resembles the design of the <a href="/wiki/Vickers_VC10" title="Vickers VC10">Vickers VC10</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Airbus devised a three-pronged main undercarriage design philosophy encompassing both four- and six-wheel <a href="/wiki/Bogie#Bogie_(aircraft)" title="Bogie">bogies</a> to ensure it can keep the pavement loading within limits. The A350-900 will have four-wheel bogies in a 4.1&#160;m (13&#160;ft) long bay. The higher weight variant, the A350-1000 (and the A350-900R, which is being proposed to <a href="/wiki/British_Airways" title="British Airways">British Airways</a>, as the \xe2\x88\x92900 size but with sufficient fuel capacity to allow nonstop London-Sydney flights) will use a six-wheel bogie, with a 4.7&#160;m (15&#160;ft) undercarriage bay.<sup id="cite_ref-landing_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-landing-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> French-based <a href="/wiki/Messier-Dowty" class="mw-redirect" title="Messier-Dowty">Messier-Dowty</a> will provide the main undercarriage for the -900 variant, and UTC Aerospace Systems will supply the \xe2\x88\x921000 variant. The nose gear will be supplied by <a href="/wiki/Liebherr_Group" title="Liebherr Group">Liebherr Aerospace</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-messier_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-messier-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wing">Wing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A350_XWB&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Wing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DSC_8030-F-WZGG_-_MSN_003_(10512622646).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/DSC_8030-F-WZGG_-_MSN_003_%2810512622646%29.jpg/220px-DSC_8030-F-WZGG_-_MSN_003_%2810512622646%29.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/DSC_8030-F-WZGG_-_MSN_003_%2810512622646%29.jpg/330px-DSC_8030-F-WZGG_-_MSN_003_%2810512622646%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/DSC_8030-F-WZGG_-_MSN_003_%2810512622646%29.jpg/440px-DSC_8030-F-WZGG_-_MSN_003_%2810512622646%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3907" data-file-height="2604" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DSC_8030-F-WZGG_-_MSN_003_(10512622646).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Airbus A350\'s blended winglets</div></div></div>\n<p>The A350 features new composite wings with a <a href="/wiki/Wingspan" title="Wingspan">wingspan</a> that is common to the proposed variants.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> Its 64.75&#160;m (212.4&#160;ft) wingspan stays within the same <a href="/wiki/ICAO#Aerodrome_Reference_Code" class="mw-redirect" title="ICAO">ICAO Aerodrome Reference Code</a> E 65m limit as the <a href="/wiki/A330" class="mw-redirect" title="A330">A330</a>/<a href="/wiki/A340" class="mw-redirect" title="A340">A340</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_777" title="Boeing 777">Boeing 777</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> The A350\'s wing has a 31.9\xc2\xb0 <a href="/wiki/Sweep_angle" class="mw-redirect" title="Sweep angle">sweep angle</a> for a <a href="/wiki/Mach_number" title="Mach number">Mach</a>&#160;0.85 <a href="/wiki/Cruise_speed" class="mw-redirect" title="Cruise speed">cruise speed</a> and have a maximum operating speed of Mach 0.89.<sup id="cite_ref-AvWeekPilotReport_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AvWeekPilotReport-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The -900 wing covers a 442&#160;m<sup>2</sup> (4,760&#160;sq&#160;ft) area.<sup id="cite_ref-tech_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tech-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> This is between the 436.8&#160;m<sup>2</sup> (4,702&#160;sq&#160;ft) wing of the current <a href="/wiki/Boeing_777" title="Boeing 777">Boeing 777</a>-200LR/300ER and the 466.8&#160;m<sup>2</sup> (5,025&#160;sq&#160;ft) wing of the in-development <a href="/wiki/Boeing_777X" title="Boeing 777X">Boeing 777X</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AA120726_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AA120726-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> However, Boeing and Airbus doesn\'t have the same measurement area.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> The A350-1000 wing is 22.3m\xc2\xb2 (240&#160;ft\xc2\xb2) larger through a 30&#160;cm (12&#160;in) extension to the inboard sections of the fixed trailing edge.<sup id="cite_ref-Flight6jul2018_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Flight6jul2018-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A new trailing-edge <a href="/wiki/High-lift_device" title="High-lift device">high-lift device</a> has been adopted with an advanced dropped-hinge <a href="/wiki/Flap_(aircraft)" class="mw-redirect" title="Flap (aircraft)">flap</a> similar to that of the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A380" title="Airbus A380">Airbus A380</a>, which permits the gap between the trailing edge and the flap to be closed with the <a href="/wiki/Spoiler_(aeronautics)" title="Spoiler (aeronautics)">spoiler</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> It is a limited morphing wing with adaptive features for continuously optimising the wing loading to reduce fuel burn: variable <a href="/wiki/Camber_(aerodynamics)" title="Camber (aerodynamics)">camber</a> for longitudinal load control where inboard &amp; outboard flaps deflect together and differential flaps setting for lateral load control where inboard &amp; outboard flaps deflect differentially.<sup id="cite_ref-1611pres_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1611pres-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The manufacturer has extensively used computational fluid dynamics and also carried out more than 4,000 hours of low- and high-speed <a href="/wiki/Windtunnel" class="mw-redirect" title="Windtunnel">windtunnel</a> testing to refine the aerodynamic design.<sup id="cite_ref-A350_aerodynamic_configuration_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A350_aerodynamic_configuration-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> The final configuration of wing and winglet was achieved for the "Maturity Gate 5" on 17 December 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-MG5_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MG5-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Wingtip_device" title="Wingtip device">wingtip device</a> curves upwards over the final 4.4&#160;m (14&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-Gunston_p.257_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gunston_p.257-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The wings are produced in the new \xc2\xa3400M, 46,000&#160;m<sup>2</sup> (500,000&#160;sq&#160;ft) North Factory at <a href="/wiki/Hawarden_Airport" title="Hawarden Airport">Airbus Broughton</a>, employing 650 workers, in a specialist facility constructed with \xc2\xa329M of support from the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Government" title="Welsh Government">Welsh Government</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cockpit_and_avionics">Cockpit and avionics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A350_XWB&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Cockpit and avionics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Airbus_A-350_XWB_F-WWYB_cockpit_view.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Airbus_A-350_XWB_F-WWYB_cockpit_view.jpg/220px-Airbus_A-350_XWB_F-WWYB_cockpit_view.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Airbus_A-350_XWB_F-WWYB_cockpit_view.jpg/330px-Airbus_A-350_XWB_F-WWYB_cockpit_view.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Airbus_A-350_XWB_F-WWYB_cockpit_view.jpg/440px-Airbus_A-350_XWB_F-WWYB_cockpit_view.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1365" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Airbus_A-350_XWB_F-WWYB_cockpit_view.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cockpit of the Airbus A350</div></div></div>\n<p>The revised design of the A350 XWB\'s <a href="/wiki/Glass_cockpit" title="Glass cockpit">glass cockpit</a> dropped the A380-sized display and adopted 38&#160;cm (15&#160;in) <a href="/wiki/Liquid-crystal_display" title="Liquid-crystal display">liquid-crystal display</a> screens. The new six-screen configuration includes two <a href="/wiki/Electronic_centralised_aircraft_monitor" title="Electronic centralised aircraft monitor">central displays</a> mounted one above the other (the lower one above the <a href="/wiki/Thrust_lever" title="Thrust lever">thrust levers</a>) and a single (for each pilot) primary flight/navigation display, with an adjacent on-board information system screen.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Airbus says the cockpit design allows for future advances in navigation technology to be placed on the displays plus gives flexibility and capacity to <a href="/wiki/Sensor_fusion" title="Sensor fusion">upload new software and to combine data from multiple sources and sensors</a> for flight management and aircraft systems control.<sup id="cite_ref-A350_avionics_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A350_avionics-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Head-up_display" title="Head-up display">head-up display</a> is also present in the cockpit.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Avionics" title="Avionics">avionics</a> is a further development of the <a href="/wiki/Integrated_modular_avionics" title="Integrated modular avionics">integrated modular avionics</a> (IMA) concept found on the A380. The A350\'s IMA will manage up to 40 functions (versus 23 functions for the A380) such as undercarriage, fuel, pneumatics, cabin environmental systems, and fire detection.<sup id="cite_ref-Gunston_p.258_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gunston_p.258-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-A350_avionics_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A350_avionics-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> Airbus stated that the benefits includes reduced maintenance and lower weight because as the IMA replaces multiple processors and LRUs with around 50 percent fewer standard computer modules known as line-replaceable modules. The IMA runs on a 100 <a href="/wiki/Megabit_per_second" class="mw-redirect" title="Megabit per second">Mbit/s</a> network based on the <a href="/wiki/Avionics_Full-Duplex_Switched_Ethernet" title="Avionics Full-Duplex Switched Ethernet">AFDX</a> standard, as employed in the A380, in place of the architecture used on the A330/A340.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Propulsion">Propulsion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A350_XWB&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Propulsion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rolls-Royce_Trent_XWB_on_Airbus_A350-941_F-WWCF_MSN002_ILA_Berlin_2016_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Rolls-Royce_Trent_XWB_on_Airbus_A350-941_F-WWCF_MSN002_ILA_Berlin_2016_01.jpg/220px-Rolls-Royce_Trent_XWB_on_Airbus_A350-941_F-WWCF_MSN002_ILA_Berlin_2016_01.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Rolls-Royce_Trent_XWB_on_Airbus_A350-941_F-WWCF_MSN002_ILA_Berlin_2016_01.jpg/330px-Rolls-Royce_Trent_XWB_on_Airbus_A350-941_F-WWCF_MSN002_ILA_Berlin_2016_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Rolls-Royce_Trent_XWB_on_Airbus_A350-941_F-WWCF_MSN002_ILA_Berlin_2016_01.jpg/440px-Rolls-Royce_Trent_XWB_on_Airbus_A350-941_F-WWCF_MSN002_ILA_Berlin_2016_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4096" data-file-height="2734" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rolls-Royce_Trent_XWB_on_Airbus_A350-941_F-WWCF_MSN002_ILA_Berlin_2016_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rolls-Royce Trent XWB engine under an A350 wing</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Trent_XWB" title="Rolls-Royce Trent XWB">Rolls-Royce Trent XWB</a> feature a 118&#160;in (300&#160;cm) <a href="/wiki/Fan_disk" title="Fan disk">fan disk</a> diameter and the design will be based on the advanced developments of the Airbus A380 <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Trent_900" title="Rolls-Royce Trent 900">Trent 900</a> and the Boeing 787 <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Trent_1000" title="Rolls-Royce Trent 1000">Trent 1000</a>. It has two thrust levels to power the A350 variants: a 84,000&#160;lbf (370&#160;kN) for the A350-900 and a 97,000&#160;lbf (430&#160;kN) for the A350-1000. The higher-thrust version will have some modifications to the fan module\xe2\x80\x94it will be the same diameter but will run slightly faster and have a new fan blade design\xe2\x80\x94and run at increased temperatures allowed by new materials technologies from Rolls-Royce\'s research.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Trent XWB may also benefit from the next-generation reduced acoustic mode scattering engine duct system (RAMSES), an <a href="/wiki/Acoustic_quieting" title="Acoustic quieting">acoustic quieting</a> engine <a href="/wiki/Nacelle" title="Nacelle">nacelle</a> intake, and a carry-on design of the Airbus\'s "zero splice" intake liner developed for the A380.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> A "<a href="/wiki/Hot_and_high" title="Hot and high">hot and high</a>" rating option for Middle Eastern customers Qatar Airways, Emirates, and <a href="/wiki/Etihad_Airways" title="Etihad Airways">Etihad Airways</a> keep its thrust available at higher temperatures and altitudes.\n</p><p>Airbus is aiming to certify the A350 with 350-minute <a href="/wiki/ETOPS" title="ETOPS">ETOPS</a> capability on entry into service.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> That could reach 420&#160;min later.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Engine <a href="/wiki/Thrust_reversal" title="Thrust reversal">thrust-reversers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nacelle" title="Nacelle">nacelles</a> will be supplied by US-based <a href="/wiki/UTC_Aerospace_Systems" title="UTC Aerospace Systems">UTC Aerospace Systems</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Systems">Systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Airbus_A350_XWB&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Honeywell" title="Honeywell">Honeywell</a> supplies its 1,700&#160;hp (1,268&#160;kW) HGT1700 <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_power_unit" title="Auxiliary power unit">auxiliary power unit</a> with 10 percent greater <a href="/wiki/Power_density" title="Power density">power density</a> than the <a href="/wiki/TPE331" class="mw-redirect" title="TPE331">TPE331</a> from which it is developed, and the air management system: the bleed air, environmental control, cabin pressure control and supplemental cooling systems.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Airbus says that the new design provides a better cabin atmosphere with 20 percent humidity, a typical <a href="/wiki/Cabin_altitude" class="mw-redirect" title="Cabin altitude">cabin altitude</a> at or below 6,000&#160;ft (1,800&#160;m) and an airflow management system that adapts cabin airflow to passenger load with draught-free air circulation.<sup id="cite_ref-presentation_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-presentation-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ram_air_turbine" title="Ram air turbine">ram air turbine</a>, capable of generating 100 <a href="/wiki/Kilovolt-ampere" class="mw-redirect" title="Kilovolt-ampere">kilovolt-ampere</a>, is supplied by <a href="/wiki/Hamilton_Sundstrand" title="Hamilton Sundstrand">Hamilton Sundstrand</a> and located in the lower surface of the fuselage.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> In light of the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_787_Dreamliner_battery_problems" title="Boeing 787 Dreamliner battery problems">Boeing 787 Dreamliner battery problems</a>, in February 2013 Airbus decided to revert from <a href="/wiki/Lithium-ion" class="mw-redirect" title="Lithium-ion">lithium-ion</a> to the proven <a href="/wiki/Nickel-cadmium" class="mw-redirect" title="Nickel-cadmium">nickel-cadmium</a> technology although the flight test programme will continue with the lithium-ion battery systems.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> In late 2015, MSN24 was delivered with 176&#160;lb (80&#160;kg) lighter <a href="/wiki/Saft_Groupe_S.A." title="Saft Groupe S.A.">Saft</a> Li-ion batteries and in June 2017, fifty A350s were flying with them and benefiting from a two-year <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_maintenance" title="Aircraft maintenance">maintenance</a> schedule instead of NiCd\xe2\x80\x99s 4\xe2\x80\x936 months.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Parker_Hannifin" title="Parker Hannifin">Parker Hannifin</a> supplies the complete fuel package: inerting system, fuel measurement and management systems, mechanical equipment and fuel pumps.
